| [![Image 2](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Alexander_von_Humboldt_signature.svg/250px-Alexander_von_Humboldt_signature.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Alexander_von_Humboldt_signature.svg) |

**Friedrich Wilhelm Heinrich Alexander von Humboldt**[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-5) (14 September 1769 – 6 May 1859) was a German [polymath](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymath "Polymath"), [geographer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geographer "Geographer"), [naturalist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_history "Natural history"), [explorer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_explorers "List of explorers"), and proponent of [Romantic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanticism "Romanticism") philosophy and [science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanticism_in_science "Romanticism in science").[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-6) He was the younger brother of the Prussian minister, philosopher, and [linguist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linguistics "Linguistics")[Wilhelm von Humboldt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_von_Humboldt "Wilhelm von Humboldt") (1767–1835).[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-7)[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-8)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-9) Humboldt's quantitative work on [botanical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Botany "Botany")[geography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography "Geography") laid the foundation for the field of [biogeography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biogeography "Biogeography"), while his advocacy of long-term systematic geophysical measurement pioneered modern [geomagnetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field "Earth's magnetic field") and [meteorological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorology "Meteorology") monitoring.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-10)[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-11) Humboldt and [Carl Ritter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Ritter "Carl Ritter") are both regarded as the founders of modern geography as they established it as an independent scientific discipline.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-12)[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-13)

Between 1799 and 1804, Humboldt travelled extensively in the [Americas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas "Americas"), exploring and describing them for the first time from a non-Spanish European scientific point of view. His description of the journey was written up and published in several volumes over 21 years.

Humboldt resurrected the use of the word _cosmos_ from the ancient Greek and assigned it to his multivolume treatise, _[Kosmos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmos\_(Humboldt\_book) "Cosmos (Humboldt book)")_, in which he sought to unify diverse branches of scientific knowledge and culture. This important work also motivated a holistic perception of the universe as one interacting entity,[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Walls_2009_3%E2%80%9315-14) which introduced concepts of [ecology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecology "Ecology") leading to ideas of [environmentalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmentalism "Environmentalism"). In 1800, and again in 1831, he described scientifically, on the basis of observations generated during his travels, local impacts of development causing [human-induced climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change "Climate change").[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Paul-2017-15)[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-16)[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-17)

Humboldt is seen as "the father of ecology" and "the father of environmentalism".[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-18)[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-19)

Early life, family and education
--------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Early life, family and education")]

Alexander von Humboldt was born in Berlin in [Prussia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prussia "Prussia") on 14 September 1769.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Klencke-1853-20) He was baptized as a baby in the Lutheran faith, with the [Duke of Brunswick-Wolfenbüttel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_I,_Duke_of_Brunswick-Wolfenb%C3%BCttel "Charles I, Duke of Brunswick-Wolfenbüttel") serving as godfather.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra19553-21)

His father, Alexander Georg von Humboldt (1720-1779), belonged to a prominent [German noble family](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_nobility "German nobility") from [Pomerania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pomerania "Pomerania"). Although not one of the titled gentry, he was a major in the [Prussian Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prussian_Army "Prussian Army"), who had served with the [Duke of Brunswick](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_William_Ferdinand,_Duke_of_Brunswick "Charles William Ferdinand, Duke of Brunswick").[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra19554%E2%80%935-22) At age 42, Alexander Georg was rewarded for his services in the [Seven Years' War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War "Seven Years' War") with the post of royal [chamberlain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chamberlain_(office) "Chamberlain (office)").[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23) He profited from the contract to lease state lotteries and tobacco sales.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra19555-24)

Alexander's grandfather was Johann Paul von Humboldt (1684-1740), who married Sophia Dorothea von Schweder (1688-1749), daughter of Prussian General Adjutant Michael von Schweder (1663-1729).[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Klencke-1853-20)[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-25) In 1766, his father, Alexander Georg married [Maria Elisabeth Colomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marie-Elisabeth_von_Humboldt "Marie-Elisabeth von Humboldt"), a well-educated woman and widow of Baron Friedrich Ernst von Holwede (1723-1765), with whom she had a son Heinrich Friedrich Ludwig (1762-1817). Alexander Georg and Maria Elisabeth had four children: two daughters, Karoline and Gabriele, who died young, and then two sons, Wilhelm and Alexander. Her first-born son, Wilhelm and Alexander's half-brother, [Rittmaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rittmaster "Rittmaster") in the [Gendarme regiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gendarme_(historical) "Gendarme (historical)") was something of a [ne'er-do-well](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ne%27er-do-well "Ne'er-do-well"), not often mentioned in the family history.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra19556%E2%80%937-26)

Alexander Georg died in 1779, leaving the brothers Humboldt in the care of their emotionally distant mother. She had high ambitions for Alexander and his older brother Wilhelm, hiring excellent tutors, who were [Enlightenment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment "Age of Enlightenment") thinkers, including Kantian physician [Marcus Herz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marcus_Herz "Marcus Herz") and botanist [Carl Ludwig Willdenow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Ludwig_Willdenow "Carl Ludwig Willdenow"), who became one of the most important botanists in Germany.[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolsonWilson1995xvi-27) Humboldt's mother expected them to become civil servants of the Prussian state.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201513,_17-28) The money left to Alexander's mother by Baron Holwede became instrumental in funding Alexander's explorations after her death; contributing more than 70% of his private income.

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Schloss_Tegel1.JPG/330px-Schloss_Tegel1.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Schloss_Tegel1.JPG)

[Schloss Tegel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schloss_Tegel "Schloss Tegel"), Berlin, where Alexander and his brother [Wilhelm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_von_Humboldt "Wilhelm von Humboldt") lived for several years

Due to his youthful penchant for collecting and labeling plants, shells, and insects, Alexander received the playful title of "the little apothecary".[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23) Marked for a political career, Alexander studied [finance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finance "Finance") for six months in 1787 at the [University of Frankfurt (Oder)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viadrina_European_University "Viadrina European University"), which his mother might have chosen less for its academic excellence than its closeness to their home in Berlin.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201517-29) On 25 April 1789, he matriculated at the [University of Göttingen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen "University of Göttingen"), then known for the lectures of [C. G. Heyne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Gottlob_Heyne "Christian Gottlob Heyne") and anatomist [J. F. Blumenbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Blumenbach "Johann Friedrich Blumenbach").[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolsonWilson1995xvi-27) His brother Wilhelm was already a student at Göttingen, but they did not interact much, since their intellectual interests were quite different.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201518-30) His vast and varied interests were by this time fully developed.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23)

At the University of Göttingen, Humboldt met Steven Jan van Geuns, a Dutch medical student, with whom he travelled to the [Rhine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhine "Rhine") in the fall of 1789. In Mainz, they met [Georg Forster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Forster "Georg Forster"), a naturalist who had been with Captain [James Cook](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Cook "James Cook") on his second voyage.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolsonWilson1995xvi%E2%80%93xv-31) Humboldt's scientific excursion resulted in his 1790 treatise _[Mineralogische Beobachtungen über einige Basalte am Rhein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mineralogische\_Beobachtungen\_%C3%BCber\_einige\_Basalte\_am\_Rhein "Mineralogische Beobachtungen über einige Basalte am Rhein")_ (Brunswick, 1790) (_Mineralogic Observations on Several Basalts on the River Rhine_).[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaum2024a19%E2%80%9322-32) The following year, 1790, Humboldt returned to Mainz to embark with Forster on a journey to England, Humboldt's first sea voyage, the Netherlands, and France.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201518-30)[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolsonWilson1995xv-33) In England, he met Sir [Joseph Banks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Banks "Joseph Banks"), president of the [Royal Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society"), who had travelled with Captain Cook; Banks showed Humboldt his huge [herbarium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbarium "Herbarium"), with specimens of the South Sea tropics.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolsonWilson1995xv-33) The scientific friendship between Banks and Humboldt lasted until Banks's death in 1820, and the two shared botanical specimens for study. Banks also mobilized his scientific contacts in later years to aid Humboldt's work.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201576,_136-34) In Paris, Humboldt and Forster witnessed the preparations for the [Festival of the Federation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/F%C3%AAte_de_la_F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration "Fête de la Fédération"). Yet, Humboldt's take on the French Revolution remained ambivalent.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaum2025-35)[_[page needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing\_sources "Wikipedia:Citing sources")_]

Humboldt's passion for travel was of long standing. He devoted to prepare himself as a scientific explorer. With this emphasis, he studied commerce and foreign languages at [Hamburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamburg "Hamburg"), geology at [Freiberg School of Mines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freiberg_University_of_Mining_and_Technology "Freiberg University of Mining and Technology") in 1791 under [A.G. Werner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abraham_Gottlob_Werner "Abraham Gottlob Werner"), leader of the [Neptunist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptunist "Neptunist") school of geology;[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolsonWilson1995lxvii-36) from anatomy at [Jena](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Jena "University of Jena") under [J.C. Loder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Justus_Christian_Loder "Justus Christian Loder"); and astronomy and the use of scientific instruments under [F.X. von Zach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franz_Xaver_von_Zach "Franz Xaver von Zach") and [J.G. Köhler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Koehler "Johann Gottfried Koehler").[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23) At Freiberg, he met a number of men who were to prove important to him in his later career, including Spaniard Manuel del Río, who became director of the School of Mines the crown established in Mexico; [Christian Leopold von Buch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Leopold_von_Buch "Christian Leopold von Buch"), who became a regional geologist; and, most importantly, [Carl Freiesleben](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Carl_Freiesleben "Johann Carl Freiesleben")[[de](https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Carl_Freiesleben "de:Johann Carl Freiesleben")], who became Humboldt's tutor and close friend. During this period, his brother Wilhelm married, but Alexander did not attend the nuptials.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195551-37)

Travels and work in Europe
--------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Travels and work in Europe")]

Humboldt graduated from the Freiberg School of Mines in 1792 and was appointed to a [Prussian government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principality_of_Bayreuth#Younger_line "Principality of Bayreuth") position in the Department of Mines as an inspector in [Bayreuth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bayreuth "Bayreuth") and the [Fichtel Mountains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fichtel_Mountains "Fichtel Mountains"). Humboldt was excellent at his job, with production of gold ore in his first year outstripping the previous eight years.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195553-38) During his period as a mine inspector, Humboldt demonstrated his deep concern for the men laboring in the mines. He opened a free school for miners, paid for out of his own pocket, which became an unchartered government training school for labor. He also sought to establish an emergency relief fund for miners, aiding them following accidents.[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195554%E2%80%9355-39)

Humboldt's researches into the vegetation of the mines of [Freiberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freiberg "Freiberg") led to the publication in Latin (1793) of his _Florae Fribergensis, accedunt Aphorismi ex Doctrina, Physiologiae Chemicae Plantarum_, which was a compendium of his botanical researches.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolsonWilson1995lxvii-36) That publication brought him to the attention of [Johann Wolfgang von Goethe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe "Johann Wolfgang von Goethe"), who had met Humboldt at the family home when Alexander was a boy, but Goethe was now interested in meeting the young scientist to discuss metamorphism of plants.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195518,_57-40) An introduction was arranged by Humboldt's brother, who lived in the university town of Jena, not far from Goethe. Goethe had developed his own extensive theories on comparative anatomy. Working before Darwin, he believed that animals had an internal force, an _urform_, that gave them a basic shape and then they were further adapted to their environment by an external force. Humboldt urged him to publish his theories. Together, the two discussed and expanded these ideas. Goethe and Humboldt soon became close friends.

Humboldt often returned to Jena in the years that followed. Goethe remarked about Humboldt to friends that he had never met anyone so versatile. Humboldt's drive served as an inspiration for Goethe. In 1797, Humboldt returned to Jena for three months. During this time, Goethe moved from his residence in Weimar to reside in Jena. Together, Humboldt and Goethe attended university lectures on anatomy and conducted their own experiments. One experiment involved hooking up a frog leg to various metals. They found no effect until the moisture of Humboldt's breath triggered a reaction that caused the frog leg to leap off the table. Humboldt described this as one of his favorite experiments because it was as if he were "breathing life into" the leg.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_May_2021]]%3Csup_class=%22noprint_Inline-Template_%22_style=%22white-space:nowrap;%22%3E&#91;%3Ci%3E[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|%3Cspan_title=%22This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&#32;(May_2021)%22%3Epage&nbsp;needed%3C/span%3E]]%3C/i%3E&#93;%3C/sup%3E-41)

During this visit, a thunderstorm killed a farmer and his wife. Humboldt obtained their corpses and analyzed them in the anatomy tower of the university.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015-42)

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Weimarer_Klassik.jpg/250px-Weimarer_Klassik.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Weimarer_Klassik.jpg)

Schiller, Wilhelm, and Alexander von Humboldt with Goethe in [Jena](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jena "Jena")

In 1794, Humboldt was admitted to the famous group of intellectuals and cultural leaders of [Weimar Classicism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weimar_Classicism "Weimar Classicism"). Goethe and [Schiller](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller "Friedrich Schiller") were the key figures at the time. Humboldt contributed (7 June 1795) to Schiller's new periodical, _Die Horen_, a philosophical [allegory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allegory "Allegory") entitled _Die Lebenskraft, oder der rhodische Genius_ _(The Life Force, or the Rhodian Genius)._[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23) In this short piece, the only literary story Humboldt ever authored, he tried to summarize the often contradictory results of the thousands of Galvanic experiments he had undertaken.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaum2019-43)

In 1792 and 1797, Humboldt was in [Vienna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vienna "Vienna"); in 1795 he made a geological and botanical tour through Switzerland and Italy. Although this service to the state was regarded by him as only an apprenticeship to the service of science, he fulfilled its duties with such conspicuous ability that not only did he rise rapidly to the highest post in his department, but he was also entrusted with several important diplomatic missions.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23)

Neither brother attended the funeral of their mother on 19 November 1796.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201539-44) Humboldt had not hidden his aversion to his mother, with one correspondent writing of him after her death, "her death... must be particularly welcomed by you".[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-45) After severing his official connections, he awaited an opportunity to fulfill his long-cherished dream of travel.

Humboldt was able to spend more time on writing up his research. He had used his own body for experimentation on muscular irritability, recently discovered by [Luigi Galvani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luigi_Galvani "Luigi Galvani") and published his results, _Versuche über die gereizte Muskel- und Nervenfaser_ (Berlin, 1797) (_Experiments on Stimulated Muscle and Nerve Fibres_), enriched in the French translation with notes by Blumenbach.

Spanish American expedition, 1799–1804
--------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Spanish American expedition, 1799–1804")]

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Map_Alexander_von_Humboldt_expedition-en.svg/330px-Map_Alexander_von_Humboldt_expedition-en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Map_Alexander_von_Humboldt_expedition-en.svg)

Alexander von Humboldt's Latin American expedition

### Seeking a foreign expedition

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Seeking a foreign expedition")]

With the financial resources to fund his scientific travels, he sought a ship on a major expedition. In the meantime, he went to Paris, where his brother Wilhelm was living. Paris was a great center of scientific learning and his brother and sister-in-law Caroline were well connected in those circles. [Louis-Antoine de Bougainville](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis-Antoine_de_Bougainville "Louis-Antoine de Bougainville") urged Humboldt to accompany him on a major expedition, likely to last five years, but the French revolutionary [Directoire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Directoire "Directoire") placed [Nicolas Baudin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicolas_Baudin "Nicolas Baudin") at the head of it rather than the aging scientific traveler.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195580-46) On the postponement of Captain Baudin's proposed voyage of [circumnavigation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumnavigation "Circumnavigation") due to continuing warfare in Europe, which Humboldt had been officially invited to accompany, Humboldt was deeply disappointed. He had already selected scientific instruments for his voyage. He did, however, have a stroke of luck with meeting [Aimé Bonpland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aim%C3%A9_Bonpland "Aimé Bonpland"), the botanist and physician for the voyage.

Discouraged, the two left Paris for [Marseille](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marseille "Marseille"), where they hoped to join [Napoleon Bonaparte](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France "Napoleon I of France") in Egypt, but North Africans were in revolt against the French invasion in Egypt and French authorities refused permission to travel. Humboldt and Bonpland eventually found their way to [Madrid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madrid "Madrid"), where their luck changed spectacularly.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195583-47)

### Spanish royal authorization, 1799

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Spanish royal authorization, 1799")]

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Carlos_IV_de_rojo.jpg/330px-Carlos_IV_de_rojo.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carlos_IV_de_rojo.jpg)

[Charles IV of Spain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_IV_of_Spain "Charles IV of Spain") who authorized Humboldt's travels and research in Spanish America

In Madrid, Humboldt sought authorization to travel to Spain's realms in the Americas; he was aided in obtaining it by the German representative of Saxony at the royal Bourbon court. Baron Forell had an interest in mineralogy and science endeavors and was inclined to help Humboldt.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195583-47) At that time, the [Bourbon Reforms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bourbon_Reforms "Bourbon Reforms") sought to reform administration of the realms and revitalize their economies.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-48) At the same time, the [Spanish Enlightenment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_Enlightenment "Spanish Enlightenment") was in florescence. For Humboldt "the confluent effect of the Bourbon revolution in government and the Spanish Enlightenment had created ideal conditions for his venture".[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991517-49)

The Bourbon monarchy had already authorized and funded expeditions, with the [Botanical Expedition to the Viceroyalty of Peru](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Botanical_Expedition_to_the_Viceroyalty_of_Peru "Botanical Expedition to the Viceroyalty of Peru") to Chile and Peru (1777–88), New Granada (1783–1816), New Spain (Mexico) (1787–1803), and the [Malaspina Expedition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malaspina_Expedition "Malaspina Expedition") (1789–94). These were lengthy, state-sponsored enterprises to gather information about plants and animals from the Spanish realms, assess economic possibilities, and provide plants and seeds for the Royal Botanical Garden in Madrid (founded 1755).[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBleichmar20125-50) These expeditions took naturalists and artists, who created visual images as well as careful written observations as well as collecting seeds and plants themselves.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBleichmar201219-51) Crown officials as early as 1779 issued and systematically distributed _Instructions concerning the most secure and economic means to transport live plants by land and sea from the most distant countries_, with illustrations, including one for the crates to transport seeds and plants.[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-52)

When Humboldt requested authorization from the crown to travel to Spanish America, most importantly, with his own financing, it was given positive response. Spain under the Habsburg monarchy had guarded its realms against foreigner travelers and intruders. The Bourbon monarch was open to Humboldt's proposal. Spanish Foreign Minister Don [Mariano Luis de Urquijo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mariano_Luis_de_Urquijo "Mariano Luis de Urquijo") received the formal proposal and Humboldt was presented to the monarch in March 1799.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195583-47) Humboldt was granted access to crown officials and written documentation on Spain's empire. With Humboldt's experience working for the absolutist Prussian monarchy as a government mining official, Humboldt had both the academic training and experience of working well within a bureaucratic structure.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991517-49)

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Alexander_von_Humboldt_by_Friedrich_Georg_Weitsch.jpg/330px-Alexander_von_Humboldt_by_Friedrich_Georg_Weitsch.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Alexander_von_Humboldt_by_Friedrich_Georg_Weitsch.jpg)

Portrait of Alexander von Humboldt by [Friedrich Georg Weitsch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Georg_Weitsch "Friedrich Georg Weitsch"), 1806

Before leaving Madrid in 1799, Humboldt and Bonpland visited the [Natural History Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Museo_Nacional_de_Ciencias_Naturales "Museo Nacional de Ciencias Naturales"), which held results of [Martín Sessé y Lacasta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mart%C3%ADn_Sess%C3%A9_y_Lacasta "Martín Sessé y Lacasta") and [José Mariano Mociño](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mariano_Moci%C3%B1o "José Mariano Mociño")'s botanical expedition to [New Spain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Spain "New Spain").[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-53) Humboldt and Bonpland met [Hipólito Ruiz López](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hip%C3%B3lito_Ruiz_L%C3%B3pez "Hipólito Ruiz López") and [José Antonio Pavón y Jiménez](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Antonio_Pav%C3%B3n_y_Jim%C3%A9nez "José Antonio Pavón y Jiménez") of the royal expedition to Peru and Chile in person in Madrid and examined their botanical collections.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-54)

### Venezuela, 1799–1800

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: Venezuela, 1799–1800")]

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Humboldt_and_Bonplant_in_the_Jungle.jpg/250px-Humboldt_and_Bonplant_in_the_Jungle.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Humboldt_and_Bonplant_in_the_Jungle.jpg)

Humboldt and [Aimé Bonpland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aim%C3%A9_Bonpland "Aimé Bonpland") were in the Amazon rainforest by the [Casiquiare River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Casiquiare_River "Casiquiare River"), with their scientific instruments, which enabled them to take many types of accurate measurements throughout their five-year journey. Oil painting by [Eduard Ender](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eduard_Ender "Eduard Ender"), 1856. Humboldt did not like the painting as the instruments depicted were inaccurate.[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-55)

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Canal_do_Cassiquiare.jpg/330px-Canal_do_Cassiquiare.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Canal_do_Cassiquiare.jpg)

Map of the [Casiquiare canal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Casiquiare_canal "Casiquiare canal") based on Humboldt's 1799 observations

Armed with authorization from the King of Spain, Humboldt and Bonpland made haste to sail, taking the ship _Pizarro_ from [A Coruña](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Coru%C3%B1a "A Coruña"), on 5 June 1799. The ship stopped six days on the island of [Tenerife](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tenerife "Tenerife"), where Humboldt climbed the volcano [Teide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teide "Teide"), and then sailed on to the New World, landing at [Cumaná](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuman%C3%A1 "Cumaná"), [Venezuela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venezuela "Venezuela"), on 16 July.

The ship's destination was not originally Cumaná, but an outbreak of typhoid on board meant that the captain changed course from [Havana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Havana "Havana") to land in northern South America. Humboldt had not mapped out a specific plan of exploration, so that the change did not upend a fixed itinerary. He later wrote that the diversion to Venezuela made possible his explorations along the Orinoco River to the border of Portuguese Brazil. With the diversion, the _Pizarro_ encountered two large dugout canoes each carrying 18 Guayaqui Indians. The _Pizarro_'s captain accepted the offer of one of them to serve as pilot. Humboldt hired this Indian, named Carlos del Pino, as a guide.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195591%E2%80%9392-56)

Venezuela from the 16th to the 18th centuries was a relative backwater compared to the seats of the Spanish viceroyalties based in New Spain (Mexico) and Peru, but during the Bourbon reforms, the northern portion of Spanish South America was reorganized administratively, with the 1777 establishment of a captaincy-general based at Caracas. A great deal of information on the new jurisdiction had already been compiled by François de Pons, but was not published until 1806.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991517-49)[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-57)

Rather than describe the administrative center of Caracas, Humboldt started his researches with the valley of Aragua, where export crops of sugar, coffee, cacao, and cotton were cultivated. Cacao plantations were the most profitable, as world demand for chocolate rose.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991518-58) It is here that Humboldt is said to have developed his idea of human-induced climate change. Investigating evidence of a rapid fall in the water level of the valley's Lake Valencia, Humboldt credited the desiccation to the clearance of tree cover and to the inability of the exposed soils to retain water. With their clear cutting of trees, the agriculturalists were removing the woodland's "threefold" moderating influence upon temperature: cooling shade, evaporation and radiation.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201556%E2%80%9359-59)

Humboldt visited the mission at [Caripe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caripe "Caripe") and explored the [Guácharo cavern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cueva_del_Gu%C3%A1charo_National_Park "Cueva del Guácharo National Park"), where he found the [oilbird](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oilbird "Oilbird"), which he was to make known to science as _Steatornis caripensis_. He also described the [Guanoco](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Bermudez "Lake Bermudez") asphalt lake as "The spring of the good priest" ("_Quelle des guten Priesters_").[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-60)[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-61) Returning to Cumaná, Humboldt observed, on the night of 11–12 November, a remarkable [meteor shower](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteor_shower "Meteor shower") (the [Leonids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonids "Leonids")). He proceeded with Bonpland to [Caracas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caracas "Caracas") where he climbed the [Avila mount](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_%C3%81vila_National_Park "El Ávila National Park") with the young poet [Andrés Bello](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Bello "Andrés Bello"), the former tutor of [Simón Bolívar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar "Simón Bolívar"), who later became the leader of independence in northern South America. Humboldt met the Venezuelan Bolívar himself in 1804 in Paris and spent time with him in Rome. The documentary record does not support the supposition that Humboldt inspired Bolívar to participate in the struggle for independence, but it does indicate Bolívar's admiration for Humboldt's production of new knowledge on Spanish America.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955232%E2%80%93233-62)

In February 1800, Humboldt and Bonpland left the coast with the purpose of exploring the course of the [Orinoco River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orinoco_River "Orinoco River") and its tributaries. This trip, which lasted four months and covered 1,725 miles (2,776 km) of wild and largely uninhabited country, had an aim of establishing the existence of the [Casiquiare canal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Casiquiare_canal "Casiquiare canal") (a communication between the water systems of the rivers Orinoco and [Amazon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazon_River "Amazon River")). Although, unbeknownst to Humboldt, this existence had been established decades before,[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_January_2021]]%3Csup_class=%22noprint_Inline-Template_%22_style=%22white-space:nowrap;%22%3E&#91;%3Ci%3E[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|%3Cspan_title=%22This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&#32;(January_2021)%22%3Epage&nbsp;needed%3C/span%3E]]%3C/i%3E&#93;%3C/sup%3E-63) his expedition had the important results of determining the exact position of the [bifurcation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/River_bifurcation "River bifurcation"),[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23) and documenting the life of several native tribes such as the Maipures and their extinct rivals the Atures (several words of the latter tribe were transferred to Humboldt by one parrot[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-64)). Around 19 March 1800, Humboldt and Bonpland discovered dangerous [electric eels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_eel "Electric eel"), whose shock could kill a man. To catch them, locals suggested they drive wild horses into the river, which brought the eels out from the river mud, and resulted in a violent confrontation of eels and horses, some of which died. Humboldt and Bonpland captured and dissected some eels, which retained their ability to shock; both received potentially dangerous electric shocks during their investigations. The encounter made Humboldt think more deeply about electricity and magnetism, typical of his ability to extrapolate from an observation to more general principles.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-65) Humboldt returned to the incident in several of his later writings, including his travelogue _Personal Narrative_ (1814–29), _Views of Nature_ (1807), and _Aspects of Nature_ (1849).[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-66)

Two months later, they explored the territory of the Maipures and that of the then-recently extinct Atures Indians. Humboldt laid to rest the persistent myth of [Walter Raleigh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Raleigh "Walter Raleigh")'s [Lake Parime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Parime "Lake Parime") by proposing that the seasonal flooding of the [Rupununi savannah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rupununi_savannah "Rupununi savannah") had been misidentified as a lake.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-67)

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Chiranthodendron_pentadactylon_monochrome.jpeg/250px-Chiranthodendron_pentadactylon_monochrome.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Chiranthodendron_pentadactylon_monochrome.jpeg)

Humboldt botanical drawing published in his work on Cuba

On 24 November 1800, the two friends set sail for Cuba, landing on 19 December,[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolsonWilson1995lxviii-68) where they met fellow botanist and [plant collector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plant_collector "Plant collector")[John Fraser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Fraser_(botanist) "John Fraser (botanist)").[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-69) Fraser and his son had been shipwrecked off the Cuban coast, and did not have a license to be in the Spanish Indies. Humboldt, who was already in Cuba, interceded with crown officials in Havana, as well as giving them money and clothing. Fraser obtained permission to remain in Cuba and explore. Humboldt entrusted Fraser with taking two cases of Humboldt and Bonpland's botanical specimens to England when he returned, for eventual conveyance to the German botanist Willdenow in Berlin.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955116%E2%80%93117-70) Humboldt and Bonpland stayed in Cuba until 5 March 1801, when they left for the mainland of northern South America again, arriving there on 30 March.

Humboldt is considered to be the "second discoverer of Cuba" due to the scientific and social research he conducted on this Spanish colony. During an initial three-month stay at [Havana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Havana "Havana"), his first tasks were to survey that city properly and the nearby towns of [Guanabacoa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guanabacoa "Guanabacoa"), [Regla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regla "Regla"), and [Bejucal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bejucal "Bejucal"). He befriended Cuban landowner and thinker [Francisco de Arango y Parreño](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francisco_de_Arango_y_Parre%C3%B1o "Francisco de Arango y Parreño"); together they visited the _Guines_ area in south Havana, the valleys of [Matanzas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matanzas "Matanzas") Province, and the [Valley of the Sugar Mills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valley_of_the_Sugar_Mills "Valley of the Sugar Mills") in [Trinidad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trinidad,_Cuba "Trinidad, Cuba"). Those three areas were, at the time, the first frontier of sugar production in the island. During those trips, Humboldt collected statistical information on Cuba's population, production, technology and trade, and with Arango, made suggestions for enhancing them. He predicted that the agricultural and commercial potential of Cuba was huge and could be vastly improved with proper leadership in the future.

On their way back to Europe from the Americas, Humboldt and Bonpland stopped again in Cuba, leaving from the port of Veracruz and arriving in Cuba on 7 January 1804, staying until 29 April 1804. In Cuba, he collected plant material and made extensive notes. During this time, he socialized with his scientific and landowner friends, conducted mineralogical surveys, and finished his vast collection of the island's flora and fauna that he eventually published as _Essai politique sur l'îsle de Cuba_.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumboldt_chronologylxix-71)

### The Andes, 1801–1803

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: The Andes, 1801–1803")]

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Humboldt-Bonpland_Chimborazo.jpg/500px-Humboldt-Bonpland_Chimborazo.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Humboldt-Bonpland_Chimborazo.jpg)

Humboldt and his fellow scientist Aimé Bonpland near the foot of the [Chimborazo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimborazo "Chimborazo") volcano, painting by [Friedrich Georg Weitsch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Georg_Weitsch "Friedrich Georg Weitsch") (1810)

After their first stay in Cuba of three months, they returned to the mainland at [Cartagena de Indias](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cartagena_de_Indias "Cartagena de Indias") (now in Colombia), a major center of trade in northern South America. Ascending the swollen stream of the [Magdalena River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magdalena_River "Magdalena River") to Honda, they arrived in Bogotá on 6 July 1801, where they met the Spanish botanist [José Celestino Mutis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Celestino_Mutis "José Celestino Mutis"), head of the Royal Botanical Expedition to New Granada, staying there until 8 September 1801. Mutis was generous with his time and gave Humboldt access to the huge pictorial record he had compiled since 1783. Mutis was based in Bogotá, but as with other Spanish expeditions, he had access to local knowledge and a workshop of artists, who created highly accurate and detailed images. This type of careful recording meant that even if specimens were not available to study at a distance, "because the images travelled, the botanists did not have to".[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBleichmar2012190-72) Humboldt was astounded at Mutis's accomplishment; when Humboldt published his first volume on botany, he dedicated it to Mutis "as a simple mark of our admiration and acknowledgement".[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-73)

Humboldt had hopes of connecting with the French sailing expedition of Baudin, now finally underway, so Bonpland and Humboldt hurried to Ecuador.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumboldt_chronologylxix-71) They crossed the frozen ridges of the [Cordillera Real](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cordillera_Real_(Ecuador) "Cordillera Real (Ecuador)") and reached [Quito](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quito "Quito") on 6 January 1802, after a tedious and difficult journey.

Their stay in Ecuador was marked by the ascent of [Pichincha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pichincha_(volcano) "Pichincha (volcano)") and their climb of [Chimborazo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimborazo "Chimborazo"), where Humboldt and his party reached an altitude of 19,286 feet (5,878 m). This was a world record at the time (for a westerner—[Incas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inca_Empire "Inca Empire") had reached much higher altitudes centuries before),[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-74) but 1000 feet short of the summit.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-75) Humboldt's journey concluded with an expedition to the sources of the Amazon _en route_ for [Lima](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lima "Lima"), Peru.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-76)

At [Callao](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Callao "Callao"), the main port for Peru, Humboldt observed the [transit of Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transit_of_Mercury "Transit of Mercury") on 9 November and studied the fertilizing properties of [guano](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guano "Guano"), rich in nitrogen, the subsequent introduction of which into Europe was due mainly to his writings.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23)

### New Spain (Mexico), 1803–1804

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: New Spain (Mexico), 1803–1804")]

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/MINA_GTO.jpg/330px-MINA_GTO.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MINA_GTO.jpg)

Silver mining complex of La Valenciana, Guanajuato, Mexico

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Vues_des_Cordill%C3%A8res%2C_et_monumens_des_peuples_indig%C3%A8nes_de_l%27Am%C3%A9rique_%281813%29_%2814781309784%29.jpg/330px-Vues_des_Cordill%C3%A8res%2C_et_monumens_des_peuples_indig%C3%A8nes_de_l%27Am%C3%A9rique_%281813%29_%2814781309784%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Vues_des_Cordill%C3%A8res,_et_monumens_des_peuples_indig%C3%A8nes_de_l%27Am%C3%A9rique_(1813)_(14781309784).jpg)

[Basalt prisms at Santa María Regla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basaltic_Prisms_of_Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_Regla "Basaltic Prisms of Santa María Regla"), Mexico by Alexander von Humboldt, published in _Vue des Cordillères et monuments des peuples indigènes de l'Amérique_

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/1479_Stein_der_f%C3%BCnften_Sonne%2C_sog._Aztekenkalender%2C_Ollin_Tonatiuh_anagoria.JPG/330px-1479_Stein_der_f%C3%BCnften_Sonne%2C_sog._Aztekenkalender%2C_Ollin_Tonatiuh_anagoria.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1479_Stein_der_f%C3%BCnften_Sonne,_sog._Aztekenkalender,_Ollin_Tonatiuh_anagoria.JPG)

Aztec calendar stone

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg/330px-Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Humboldt_1810_pp_47_48_50_51_52.jpg)

[Dresden Codex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dresden_Codex "Dresden Codex"), later identified as a Maya manuscript, published in part by Humboldt in 1810

Humboldt and Bonpland had not intended to go to New Spain, but when they were unable to join a voyage to the Pacific, they left the Ecuadorian port of Guayaquil and headed for [Acapulco](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acapulco "Acapulco") on Mexico's west coast. Even before Humboldt and Bonpland started on their way to New Spain's [capital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico_City "Mexico City") on Mexico's central plateau, Humboldt realized the captain of the vessel that brought them to Acapulco had reckoned its location incorrectly. Since Acapulco was the main west-coast port and the terminus of the [Asian trade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manila_Galleon "Manila Galleon") from the Spanish Philippines, having accurate maps of its location was extremely important. Humboldt set up his instruments, surveying the deep-water bay of Acapulco, to determine its longitude.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955149%E2%80%93150-77)[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumboldt_chronologylxviii%E2%80%93lxvix-78)

Humboldt and Bonpland landed in Acapulco on 15 February 1803, and from there they went to [Taxco](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxco "Taxco"), a silver-mining town in modern [Guerrero](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guerrero "Guerrero"). In April 1803, he visited [Cuernavaca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuernavaca "Cuernavaca"), [Morelos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morelos "Morelos"). Impressed by its climate, he nicknamed the city the _City of Eternal Spring_.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-79)[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-80) Humboldt and Bonpland arrived in Mexico City, having been officially welcomed via a letter from the king's representative in New Spain, Viceroy Don [José de Iturrigaray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_Iturrigaray "José de Iturrigaray"). Humboldt was also given a special passport to travel throughout New Spain and letters of introduction to intendants, the highest officials in New Spain's administrative districts (intendancies). This official aid to Humboldt allowed him to have access to crown records, mines, landed estates, canals, and Mexican antiquities from the prehispanic era.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955156-81) Humboldt read the writings of Bishop-elect of the important diocese of Michoacan [Manuel Abad y Queipo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manuel_Abad_y_Queipo "Manuel Abad y Queipo"), a [classical liberal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_liberal "Classical liberal"), that were directed to the crown for the improvement of New Spain.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527-82)

They spent the year in the viceroyalty, traveling to different Mexican cities in the central plateau and the northern mining region. The first journey was from Acapulco to Mexico City, through what is now the Mexican state of [Guerrero](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guerrero "Guerrero"). The route was suitable only for mule train, and all along the way, Humboldt took measurements of elevation. When he left Mexico a year later in 1804, from the east coast port of Veracruz, he took a similar set of measures, which resulted in a chart in the _Political Essay_, the physical plan of Mexico with the dangers of the road from Acapulco to Mexico City, and from Mexico City to Veracruz.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-83) This visual depiction of elevation was part of Humboldt's general insistence that the data he collected be presented in a way more easily understood than statistical charts. A great deal of his success in gaining a more general readership for his works was his understanding that "anything that has to do with extent or quantity can be represented geometrically. Statistical projections [charts and graphs], which speak to the senses without tiring the intellect have the advantage of bringing attention to a large number of important facts".[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-84)

Humboldt was impressed with Mexico City, which at the time was the largest city in the Americas, and one that could be counted as modern. He declared "no city of the new continent, without even excepting those of the United States, can display such great and solid scientific establishments as the capital of Mexico".[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-85) He pointed to the [Royal College of Mines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palacio_de_Miner%C3%ADa#History "Palacio de Minería"), the [Royal Botanical Garden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Palace_(Mexico)#Viceregal_palace "National Palace (Mexico)") and the [Royal Academy of San Carlos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Academy_of_San_Carlos "Academy of San Carlos") as exemplars of a metropolitan capital in touch with the latest developments on the continent and insisting on its modernity.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991526%E2%80%93527-86) He also recognized important [criollo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Criollo_people "Criollo people") savants in Mexico, including [José Antonio de Alzate y Ramírez](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Antonio_de_Alzate_y_Ram%C3%ADrez "José Antonio de Alzate y Ramírez"), who died in 1799, just before Humboldt's visit; Miguel Velásquez de León; and [Antonio de León y Gama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antonio_de_Le%C3%B3n_y_Gama "Antonio de León y Gama").[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527-82)

Humboldt spent time at the Valenciana silver mine in [Guanajuato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guanajuato "Guanajuato"), central New Spain, at the time the most important in the Spanish empire.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991525-87) The bicentennial of his visit in Guanajuato was celebrated with a conference at the [University of Guanajuato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Guanajuato "University of Guanajuato"), with Mexican academics highlighting various aspects of his impact on the city.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-88) Humboldt could have simply examined the geology of the fabulously rich mine, but he took the opportunity to study the entire mining complex as well as analyze mining statistics of its output. His report on silver mining is a major contribution, and considered the strongest and best informed section of his _Political Essay_. Although Humboldt was himself a trained geologist and mining inspector, he drew on mining experts in Mexico. One was [Fausto Elhuyar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fausto_Elhuyar "Fausto Elhuyar"), then head of the General Mining Court in Mexico City, who, like Humboldt was trained in Freiberg. Another was [Andrés Manuel del Río](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Manuel_del_R%C3%ADo "Andrés Manuel del Río"), director of Royal College of Mines, whom Humboldt knew when they were both students in Freiberg.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra195551,_156-89) The Bourbon monarchs had established the mining court and the college to elevate mining as a profession, since revenues from silver constituted the crown's largest source of income. Humboldt also consulted other German mining experts, who were already in Mexico.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527-82) While Humboldt was a welcome foreign scientist and mining expert, the Spanish crown had established fertile ground for Humboldt's investigations into mining.

Spanish America's ancient civilizations were a source of interest for Humboldt, who included images of Mexican manuscripts (or codices) and Inca ruins in his richly illustrated _Vues des cordillères et monuments des peuples indigènes de l'Amerique_ (1810–1813), the most experimental of Humboldt's publications, since it does not have "a single ordering principle" but his opinions and contentions based on observation.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKutzinskiEtte2012xxi-90) For Humboldt, a key question was the influence of climate on the development of these civilizations.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991523-91) When he published his _Vues des cordillères_, he included a color image of the [Aztec calendar stone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aztec_calendar_stone "Aztec calendar stone"), which had been discovered buried in the [main plaza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Z%C3%B3calo "Zócalo") of Mexico City in 1790, along with select drawings of the [Dresden Codex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dresden_Codex "Dresden Codex") and others he sought out later in European collections. His aim was to muster evidence that these pictorial and sculptural images could allow the reconstruction of prehispanic history. He sought out Mexican experts in the interpretation of sources from there, especially Antonio Pichardo, who was the literary executor of [Antonio de León y Gama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antonio_de_Le%C3%B3n_y_Gama "Antonio de León y Gama")'s work. For American-born Spaniards ([criollos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Criollo_people "Criollo people")) who were seeking sources of pride in Mexico's ancient past, Humboldt's recognition of these ancient works and dissemination in his publications was a boon. He read the work of exiled Jesuit [Francisco Javier Clavijero](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francisco_Javier_Clavijero "Francisco Javier Clavijero"), which celebrated Mexico's prehispanic civilization, and which Humboldt invoked to counter the pejorative assertions about the new world by Buffon, de Pauw, and Raynal.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKutzinskiEtte2012xv-92) Humboldt ultimately viewed both the prehispanic realms of Mexico and Peru as despotic and barbaric.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991523%E2%80%93525-93) However, he also drew attention to indigenous monuments and artifacts as cultural productions that had "both ... historical _and_ artistic significance".[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKutzinskiEtte2012xxxiii-94)

One of his most widely read publications resulting from his travels and investigations in Spanish America was the _Essai politique sur le royaum de la Nouvelle Espagne_, quickly translated to English as _Political Essay on the Kingdom of New Spain_ (1811).[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-95) This treatise was the result of Humboldt's own investigations as well as the generosity of Spanish colonial officials for statistical data.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-96)

### The United States, 1804

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: The United States, 1804")]

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Louisiana1804a.jpg/250px-Louisiana1804a.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Louisiana1804a.jpg)

1804 map of the Louisiana Territory. Jefferson and his cabinet sought information from Humboldt when he visited Washington, D.C., about Spain's territory in Mexico, now bordering the U.S.

Leaving from Cuba, Humboldt decided to take an unplanned short visit to the United States. Knowing that the current U.S. president, [Thomas Jefferson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson"), was himself a scientist, Humboldt wrote to him saying that he would be in the United States. Jefferson warmly replied, inviting him to visit the [White House](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_House "White House") in the nation's new capital. In his letter Humboldt had gained Jefferson's interest by mentioning that he had discovered [mammoth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mammoth "Mammoth") teeth near the Equator. Jefferson had previously written that he believed mammoths had never lived so far south. Humboldt had also hinted at his knowledge of New Spain.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Schwarz-2001-97)

Arriving in [Philadelphia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia"), which was a center of learning in the U.S., Humboldt met with some of the major scientific figures of the era, including chemist and anatomist [Caspar Wistar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caspar_Wistar_(physician) "Caspar Wistar (physician)"), who pushed for compulsory smallpox vaccination, and botanist [Benjamin Smith Barton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Smith_Barton "Benjamin Smith Barton"), as well as physician [Benjamin Rush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Rush "Benjamin Rush"), a signer of the [Declaration of Independence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence "United States Declaration of Independence"), who wished to hear about [cinchona](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cinchona "Cinchona") bark from a South American tree, which cured fevers.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955175%E2%80%93176-98) Humboldt's treatise on cinchona was published in English in 1821.[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-99)

After arriving in Washington D.C, Humboldt held numerous intense discussions with Jefferson on both scientific matters and also his year-long stay in New Spain. Jefferson had only recently concluded the [Louisiana Purchase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase "Louisiana Purchase"), which now placed New Spain on the southwest border of the United States. The Spanish minister in Washington, D.C. had declined to furnish the U.S. government with information about Spanish territories, and access to the territories was strictly controlled. Humboldt was able to supply Jefferson with the latest information on the population, trade agriculture and military of New Spain. This information would later be the basis for his _Essay on the Political Kingdom of New Spain_ (1810).

Jefferson was unsure of where the border of the newly-purchased [Louisiana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louisiana "Louisiana") was precisely, and Humboldt wrote him a two-page report on the matter. Jefferson would later refer to Humboldt as "the most scientific man of the age". [Albert Gallatin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_Gallatin "Albert Gallatin"), Secretary of the Treasury, said of Humboldt "I was delighted and swallowed more information of various kinds in less than two hours than I had for two years past in all I had read or heard." Gallatin, in turn, supplied Humboldt with information he sought on the United States.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Schwarz-2001-97)

After six weeks, Humboldt set sail for Europe from the mouth of the [Delaware](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delaware_River "Delaware River") and landed at [Bordeaux](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bordeaux "Bordeaux") on 3 August 1804.

Humboldt kept a detailed diary of his sojourn to Spanish America, running some 4,000 pages, which he drew on directly for his multiple publications following the expedition. The leather-bound diaries themselves are now in Germany, having been returned from Russia to East Germany, where they were taken by the Red Army after World War II. Following German reunification, the diaries were returned to a descendant of Humboldt. For a time, there was concern about their being sold, but that was averted.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-100) A government-funded project to digitize the Spanish American expedition as well as his later Russian expedition has been undertaken (2014–2017) by the University of Potsdam and the German State Library–Prussian Cultural Heritage Foundation.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-101)

### Achievements of the Hispanic American expedition

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Achievements of the Hispanic American expedition")]

Humboldt's decades' long endeavor to publish the results of this expedition not only resulted in multiple volumes, but also made his international reputation in scientific circles. Humboldt came to be well-known with the reading public as well, with popular, densely illustrated, condensed versions of his work in multiple languages. Bonpland, his fellow scientist and collaborator on the expedition, collected botanical specimens and preserved them, but unlike Humboldt who had a passion to publish, Bonpland had to be prodded to do the formal descriptions. Many scientific travelers and explorers produced huge visual records, which remained unseen by the general public until the late nineteenth century, in the case of the Malaspina Expedition, and even the late twentieth century, when Mutis's botanical, some 12,000 drawings from New Granada, was published. Humboldt, by contrast, published immediately and continuously, using and ultimately exhausting his personal fortune, to produce both scientific and popular texts. Humboldt's name and fame were made by his travels to Spanish America, particularly his publication of the _Political Essay on the Kingdom of New Spain_. His image as the premier European scientist was a later development.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-102)

For the Bourbon crown, which had authorized the expedition, the returns were not only tremendous in terms of sheer volume of data on their New World realms, but in dispelling the vague and pejorative assessments of the New World by [Guillaume-Thomas Raynal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guillaume-Thomas_Raynal "Guillaume-Thomas Raynal"), [Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georges-Louis_Leclerc,_Comte_de_Buffon "Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon"), and [William Robertson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Robertson_(historian) "William Robertson (historian)"). The achievements of the Bourbon regime, especially in New Spain, were evident in the precise data Humboldt systematized and published.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991527-82)

This memorable expedition may be regarded as having laid the foundation of the sciences of [physical geography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_geography "Physical geography"), [plant geography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plant_geography "Plant geography"), and [meteorology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorology "Meteorology"). Key to that was Humboldt's meticulous and systematic measurement of phenomena with the most advanced instruments then available. He closely observed plant and animal species in situ, not just in isolation, noting all elements in relation to one other. He collected specimens of plants and animals, dividing the growing collection so that if a portion was lost, other parts might survive.

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG/250px-Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Frederich_heinrich_baron_von_humboldt-charles_wilson_peale.JPG)

Humboldt depicted by American artist [Charles Willson Peale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale "Charles Willson Peale"), 1805, who met Humboldt when he visited the U.S. in 1804

Humboldt saw the need for an approach to science that could account for the harmony of nature among the diversity of the physical world. For Humboldt, "the unity of nature" meant that it was the interrelation of all [physical sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_sciences "Physical sciences")—such as the conjoining between [biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology "Biology"), [meteorology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorology "Meteorology") and [geology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geology "Geology")—that determined where specific plants grew. He found these relationships by unraveling myriad, painstakingly collected data,[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHelferich200425-103) data extensive enough that it became an enduring foundation upon which others could base their work. Humboldt viewed nature [holistically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holism "Holism"), and tried to explain natural phenomena without the appeal to religious dogma. He believed in the central importance of observation, and as a consequence had amassed a vast array of the most sophisticated scientific instruments then available. Each had its own velvet lined box and was the most accurate and portable of its time; nothing quantifiable escaped measurement. According to Humboldt, everything should be measured with the finest and most modern instruments and sophisticated techniques available, for that collected data was the basis of all scientific understanding.

This quantitative methodology would become known as [Humboldtian science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humboldtian_science "Humboldtian science"). Humboldt wrote "Nature herself is sublimely eloquent. The stars as they sparkle in firmament fill us with delight and ecstasy, and yet they all move in orbit marked out with mathematical precision."[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-104) However, [Andreas Daum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andreas_Daum "Andreas Daum") has recently revisited the concept of Humboldtian Science and set it apart from "Humboldt's science".[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaum2024b29%E2%80%9351-105)

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg/250px-Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_-_Ideen_zu_einer_Geographie_der_Pflanzen_nebst_einem_Naturgem%C3%A4lde_der_Tropenl%C3%A4nder_-_000012142.jpg)

Humboldt's _Naturgemälde_, also known as the Chimborazo Map, is his depiction of the volcanoes Chimborazo and Cotopaxi in cross section, with detailed information about plant geography. The illustration was published in _The Geography of Plants_, 1807, in a large format (54 cm x 84 cm). Largely used for global warming analyses, this map depicts in fact the vegetation of another volcano: the [Antisana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antisana "Antisana").[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-106)

His _Essay on the Geography of Plants_ (published first in French and then German, both in 1807) was based on the then novel idea of studying the distribution of organic life as affected by varying physical conditions.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23) This was most famously depicted in his published cross-section of Chimborazo, approximately two feet by three feet (54 cm x 84 cm) color pictorial, he called _Ein Naturgemälde der Anden_ and what is also called the Chimborazo Map. It was a fold-out at the back of the publication.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZimmerer2011125-107) Humboldt first sketched the map when he was in South America, which included written descriptions on either side of the cross-section of Chimborazo. These detailed the information on temperature, altitude, humidity, atmosphere pressure, and the animal and plants (with their scientific names) found at each elevation. Plants from the same genus appear at different elevations. The depiction is on an east-west axis going from the Pacific coast lowlands to the Andean range of which Chimborazo was a part, and the eastern Amazonian basin. Humboldt showed the three zones of coast, mountains, and Amazonia, based on his own observations, but he also drew on existing Spanish sources, particularly [Pedro Cieza de León](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pedro_Cieza_de_Le%C3%B3n "Pedro Cieza de León"), which he explicitly referred to. The Spanish American scientist [Francisco José de Caldas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Caldas "Francisco José de Caldas") had also measured and observed mountain environments and had earlier come to similar ideas about environmental factors in the distribution of life forms.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZimmerer2011129-108) Humboldt was thus not putting forward something entirely new, but it is argued that his finding is not derivative either.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-109) The Chimborazo map displayed complex information in an accessible fashion. The map was the basis for comparison with other major peaks. "The Naturgemälde showed for the first time that nature was a global force with corresponding climate zones across continents."[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-110) Another assessment of the map is that it "marked the beginning of a new era of environmental science, not only of mountain ecology but also of global-scale biogeophysical patterns and processes."[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZimmerer2011125-107)

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg/250px-Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Woodbridge_isothermal_chart3.jpg)

Isothermal map of the world using Humboldt's data by [William Channing Woodbridge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Channing_Woodbridge "William Channing Woodbridge")

By his delineation (in 1817) of [isothermal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isothermal "Isothermal") lines, he at once suggested the idea and devised the means of comparing the climatic conditions of various countries. He first investigated the rate of decrease in mean temperature with the increase in elevation above sea level, and afforded, by his inquiries regarding the origin of tropical storms, the earliest clue to the detection of the more complicated law governing atmospheric disturbances in higher latitudes.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23)[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-111) This was a major contribution to climatology.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955380-112)[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-113)

His discovery of the decrease in intensity of Earth's [magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field "Magnetic field") from the poles to the equator was communicated to the Paris Institute in a memoir read by him on 7 December 1804. Its importance was attested by the speedy emergence of rival claims.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23)

His services to geology were based on his attentive study of the [volcanoes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcano "Volcano") of the Andes and Mexico, which he observed and sketched, climbed, and measured with a variety of instruments. By climbing Chimborazo, he established an altitude record which became the basis for measurement of other volcanoes in the Andes and the Himalayas. As with other aspects of his investigations, he developed methods to show his synthesized results visually, using the graphic method of geologic-cross sections.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955375%E2%80%93376-114) He showed that volcanoes fell naturally into linear groups, presumably corresponding with vast subterranean fissures; and by his demonstration of the [igneous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Igneous_rock "Igneous rock") origin of rocks previously held to be of aqueous formation, he contributed largely to the elimination of erroneous views, such as [Neptunism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptunism "Neptunism").[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-EB1911-23)

Humboldt was a significant contributor to cartography, creating maps, particularly of New Spain, that became the template for later mapmakers in Mexico. His careful recording of latitude and longitude led to accurate maps of Mexico, the port of Acapulco, the port of Veracruz, and the Valley of Mexico, and a map showing trade patterns among continents. His maps also included schematic information on geography, converting areas of administrative districts (intendancies) using proportional squares.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-115) The U.S. was keen to see his maps and statistics on New Spain, since they had implication for territorial claims following the Louisiana Purchase.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955177%E2%80%93178-116) Later in life, Humboldt published three volumes (1836–39) examining sources that dealt with the early voyages to the Americas, pursuing his interest in nautical astronomy in the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries. His research yielded the origin of the name "America", put on a map of the Americas by [Martin Waldseemüller](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Waldseem%C3%BCller "Martin Waldseemüller").[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-117)

[![Image 20](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png/250px-Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Head-claw-Vultur-gryphus-Humboldt-Zoologie-T09p170.png)

Humboldt's depiction of an [Andean condor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andean_condor "Andean condor"), an example of his detailed drawing
