Humboldt conducted a census of the indigenous and European inhabitants in [New Spain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Spain "New Spain"), publishing a schematized drawing of racial types and populations distribution, grouping them by region and social characteristics.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-118) He estimated the population to be six million individuals.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-119)[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-120) He estimated Indians to be forty percent of New Spain's population, but their distribution being uneven; the most dense were in the center and south of Mexico, the least dense in the north. He presented these data in chart form, for easier understanding.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-121) He also surveyed the non-Indian population, categorized as Whites (Spaniards), _Negroes_, and castes (_[castas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Casta "Casta")_).[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-122) American-born Spaniards, so-called _[creoles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Criollo\_people "Criollo people")_ had been [painting depictions of mixed-race family groupings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pintura_de_castas "Pintura de castas") in the eighteenth century, showing father of one racial category, mother of another, and the offspring in a third category in hierarchical order, so racial hierarchy was an essential way elites viewed Mexican society.[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-123) Humboldt reported that American-born Spaniards were legally racial equals of [those born in Spain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peninsulares "Peninsulares"), but the crown policy since the Bourbons took the Spanish throne privileged those born in Iberia. Humboldt observed that "the most miserable European, without education and without intellectual cultivation, thinks himself superior to whites born in the new continent".[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-124) The truth in this assertion, and the conclusions derived from them, have been often disputed as superficial, or politically motivated, by some authors, considering that between 40% and 60% of high offices in the new world were held by creoles.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-125)[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Jos%C3%A9_Oscar_Frigerio_1801-126) The enmity between some creoles and the peninsular-born whites increasingly became an issue in the late period of Spanish rule, with creoles increasingly alienated from the crown. Humboldt's assessment was that royal government abuses and the example of [a new model of rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Revolution "American Revolution") in the United States were eroding the unity of whites in New Spain.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-127) Humboldt's writings on race in New Spain were shaped by the memorials of the [classical liberal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_liberal "Classical liberal"), enlightened Bishop-elect of Michoacán, [Manuel Abad y Queipo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manuel_Abad_y_Queipo "Manuel Abad y Queipo"), who personally presented Humboldt with his printed memorials to the Spanish crown critiquing social and economic conditions and his recommendations for eliminating them.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-128)[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Jos%C3%A9_Oscar_Frigerio_1801-126)

One scholar says that his writings contain fantastical descriptions of America, while leaving out its inhabitants, stating that Humboldt, coming from the [Romantic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanticism "Romanticism") school of thought, believed '...nature is perfect till man deforms it with care'.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Pratt_1997-129) The further assessment is that he largely neglected the human societies amidst nature. Views of indigenous peoples as 'savage' or 'unimportant' leaves them out of the historical picture.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Pratt_1997-129) Other scholars counter that Humboldt dedicated large parts of his work to describing the [conditions of slaves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Spanish_Empire "Slavery in the Spanish Empire"), indigenous peoples, mixed-race [castas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Castas "Castas"), and society in general. He often showed his disgust for the slavery[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-130) and inhumane conditions in which indigenous peoples and others were treated and he often criticized Spanish colonial policies.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTERupke2008138-131)

Humboldt was not primarily an artist, but he could draw well, allowing him to record a visual record of particular places and their natural environment. Many of his drawings became the basis for illustrations of his many scientific and general publications. Artists whom Humboldt influenced, such as [Johann Moritz Rugendas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Moritz_Rugendas "Johann Moritz Rugendas"), followed in his path and painted the same places Humboldt had visited and recorded, such as the basalt formations in Mexico, which was an illustration in his _Vues des Cordillères_.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-132)[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-133)

The editing and publication of the encyclopedic mass of scientific, political and archaeological material that had been collected by him during his absence from Europe was now Humboldt's most urgent desire. After a short trip to Italy with [Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac "Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac") for the purpose of investigating the law of [magnetic declination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_declination "Magnetic declination") and a stay of two and a half years in Berlin, in the spring of 1808, he settled in Paris. His purpose for being located there was to secure the scientific cooperation required for bringing his great work through the press. This colossal task, which he at first hoped would occupy but two years, eventually cost him twenty-one, and even then it remained incomplete.

*   [![Image 21: House where Humboldt and Bonpland lived in Mexico City in 1803, located at 80 Rep. de Uruguay in the historic centre, just south of the Zocalo](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/HumboldtHouseDF.JPG/250px-HumboldtHouseDF.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:HumboldtHouseDF.JPG "House where Humboldt and Bonpland lived in Mexico City in 1803, located at 80 Rep. de Uruguay in the historic centre, just south of the Zocalo")

*   [![Image 22: Statue to Humboldt in Alameda Park, Mexico City, erected 1999 on the two hundredth-anniversary of the beginning of his travels to Spanish America](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/StatueAlexHumboldtMexico.JPG/120px-StatueAlexHumboldtMexico.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:StatueAlexHumboldtMexico.JPG "Statue to Humboldt in Alameda Park, Mexico City, erected 1999 on the two hundredth-anniversary of the beginning of his travels to Spanish America")

Statue to Humboldt in Alameda Park, Mexico City, erected 1999 on the two hundredth-anniversary of the beginning of his travels to Spanish America

*   [![Image 23: Statue of Humboldt in Cuernavaca, Mexico](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Humboldt_-_Cuernavaca_I.jpg/120px-Humboldt_-_Cuernavaca_I.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Humboldt_-_Cuernavaca_I.jpg "Statue of Humboldt in Cuernavaca, Mexico")

Statue of Humboldt in [Cuernavaca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuernavaca "Cuernavaca"), [Mexico](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico "Mexico") 
*   [![Image 24: Waterfall over the Basaltic Prisms of Santa María Regla, Huasca de Ocampo, Hidalgo, Mexico, that Humboldt sketched](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Prismas_Bas%C3%A1lticos%2C_Huasca_de_Ocampo%2C_Hidalgo%2C_M%C3%A9xico%2C_2013-10-10%2C_DD_42.JPG/120px-Prismas_Bas%C3%A1lticos%2C_Huasca_de_Ocampo%2C_Hidalgo%2C_M%C3%A9xico%2C_2013-10-10%2C_DD_42.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Prismas_Bas%C3%A1lticos,_Huasca_de_Ocampo,_Hidalgo,_M%C3%A9xico,_2013-10-10,_DD_42.JPG "Waterfall over the Basaltic Prisms of Santa María Regla, Huasca de Ocampo, Hidalgo, Mexico, that Humboldt sketched")

Waterfall over the [Basaltic Prisms of Santa María Regla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basaltic_Prisms_of_Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_Regla "Basaltic Prisms of Santa María Regla"), [Huasca de Ocampo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huasca_de_Ocampo "Huasca de Ocampo"), Hidalgo, Mexico, that Humboldt sketched 

Scholarly and public recognition
--------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Scholarly and public recognition")]

[![Image 25](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg/250px-Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Alexander_von_humboldt_1807_225-91-1-PB.jpeg)

Humboldt in Berlin 1807

During his lifetime Humboldt became one of the most famous men in Europe.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTESachs20061-134) Academies, both native and foreign, were eager to elect him to their membership, the first being The [American Philosophical Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society "American Philosophical Society")[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-135) in Philadelphia, which he visited at the tail end of his travel through the Americas. He was elected to the [Prussian Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prussian_Academy_of_Sciences "Prussian Academy of Sciences") in 1805.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955204-136)

Over the years other learned societies in the U.S. elected him a member, including the [American Antiquarian Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Antiquarian_Society "American Antiquarian Society") (Worcester, MA) in 1816;[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-137) the [Linnean Society of London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linnean_Society_of_London "Linnean Society of London") in 1818; the [New York Historical Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_York_Historical_Society "New York Historical Society") in 1820; a Foreign Honorary Member of the [American Academy of Arts and Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences "American Academy of Arts and Sciences") in 1822;[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-138) the [American Ethnological Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Ethnological_Society "American Ethnological Society") (New York) in 1843; and the American Geographical and Statistical Society, (New York) in 1856.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955377-139) He was elected a foreign member of the [Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences "Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences") in 1810. The [Royal Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society"), whose president Sir Joseph Banks had aided Humboldt as a young man, now welcomed him as a foreign member.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015166-140)

After Mexican independence from Spain in 1821, the Mexican government recognized him with high honors for his services to the nation. In 1827, the first [President of Mexico](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_Mexico "President of Mexico"), [Guadalupe Victoria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guadalupe_Victoria "Guadalupe Victoria") granted Humboldt Mexican citizenship[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-141) and in 1859, the President of Mexico, [Benito Juárez](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benito_Ju%C3%A1rez "Benito Juárez"), named Humboldt a hero of the nation (_benemérito de la nación_).[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-142) The gestures were purely honorary; he never returned to the Americas following his expedition.

Importantly for Humboldt's long-term financial stability, King [Frederick William III of Prussia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederick_William_III_of_Prussia "Frederick William III of Prussia") conferred upon him the honor of the post of royal chamberlain, without at the time exacting the duties. The appointment had a pension of 2,500 [thalers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thaler "Thaler"), afterwards doubled. This official stipend became his main source of income in later years when he exhausted his fortune on the publications of his research. Financial necessity forced his permanent relocation to Berlin in 1827 from Paris. In Paris he found not only scientific sympathy, but the social stimulus which his vigorous and healthy mind eagerly craved. He was equally in his element as the lion of the [salons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salon_(gathering) "Salon (gathering)") and as the savant of the [Institut de France](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institut_de_France "Institut de France") and the observatory.

[![Image 26](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Gedenktafel_Karolinenstr_19_%28Tegel%29_Alexander_von_Humboldt.jpg/250px-Gedenktafel_Karolinenstr_19_%28Tegel%29_Alexander_von_Humboldt.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gedenktafel_Karolinenstr_19_(Tegel)_Alexander_von_Humboldt.jpg)

Memorial plaque, Alexander von Humboldt, Karolinenstraße 19, Berlin-Tegel, Germany

On 12 May 1827 he settled permanently in Berlin, where his first efforts were directed towards the furtherance of the science of terrestrial magnetism. In 1827, he began giving public lectures in Berlin, which became the basis for his last major publication, [_Kosmos_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosmos_(Humboldt) "Kosmos (Humboldt)") (1845–62).[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumboldt_chronologylxix-71)

For many years, it had been one of his favorite schemes to secure, by means of simultaneous observations at distant points, a thorough investigation of the nature and law of "[magnetic storms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_storm "Magnetic storm")" (a term invented by him to designate abnormal disturbances of [Earth's magnetism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field "Earth's magnetic field")). The meeting at Berlin, on 18 September 1828, of a newly formed scientific association, of which he was elected president, gave him the opportunity of setting on foot an extensive system of research in combination with his diligent personal observations. His appeal to the Russian government, in 1829, led to the establishment of a line of magnetic and meteorological stations across northern Asia. Meanwhile, his letter to the [Duke of Sussex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prince_Augustus_Frederick,_Duke_of_Sussex "Prince Augustus Frederick, Duke of Sussex"), then (April 1836) president of the Royal Society, secured for the undertaking, the wide basis of the [British dominions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire "British Empire").

The [_Encyclopædia Britannica_, Eleventh Edition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition "Encyclopædia Britannica Eleventh Edition"), observes, "Thus that scientific conspiracy of nations which is one of the noblest fruits of modern civilization was by his exertions first successfully organized".[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClerke1911874-143) However, earlier examples of international scientific cooperation exist, notably the 18th-century observations of the [transits of Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transit_of_Venus "Transit of Venus").

In 1856, U.S. diplomat [John Bigelow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Bigelow "John Bigelow") published _Memoir of the Life and Public Services of [John Charles Fremont](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John\_Charles\_Fremont "John Charles Fremont")_. He dedicated it "To Alexander von Humboldt, this memoir of one whose genius he was among the first to discover and acknowledge, is respectfully inscribed by The Author."[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-144)

In 1869, the 100th year of his birth, Humboldt's fame was so great that cities all over America celebrated his birth with large festivals. In New York City, a bust of his head was unveiled in [Central Park](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Park "Central Park").[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Nichols,_Sandra_2006-145)

Scholars have speculated about the reasons for Humboldt's declining renown among the public. Sandra Nichols has argued that there are three reasons for this:

*   First, a trend towards specialization in scholarship. Humboldt was a generalist who connected many disciplines in his work. Today, academics have become more and more focused on narrow fields of work. Humboldt combined [ecology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecology "Ecology"), geography and even social sciences.
*   Second, a change in writing style. Humboldt's works, which were considered essential to a library in 1869, had flowery prose that fell out of fashion. One critic said they had a "laborious picturesqueness". Humboldt himself said that, "If I only knew how to describe adequately how and what I felt, I might, after this long journey of mine, really be able to give happiness to people. The disjointed life I lead makes me hardly certain of my way of writing".
*   Third, a rising anti-German sentiment in the late 1800s and the early 1900s due to heavy German immigration to the United States and later World War 1.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Nichols,_Sandra_2006-145) On the eve of the 1959 hundredth anniversary of the death of Humboldt, the government of West Germany planned significant celebrations in conjunction with nations that Humboldt visited.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-146)

Expedition in Russia, 1829
--------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Expedition in Russia, 1829")]

[![Image 27](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Humboldt_RUS.jpg/500px-Humboldt_RUS.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Humboldt_RUS.jpg)

Map of Humboldt's expedition to Russia in 1829

In 1811, and again in 1818, projects of Asiatic exploration were proposed to Humboldt, first by Tsar [Nicholas I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia "Nicholas I of Russia")'s Russian government, and afterwards by the Prussian government; but on each occasion, untoward circumstances interposed. It was not until he had begun his sixtieth year that he resumed his early role of traveler in the interests of science.

The Russian Foreign Minister, Count [Georg von Cancrin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_von_Cancrin "Georg von Cancrin"), contacted Humboldt about whether a [platinum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platinum "Platinum")-based currency was possible in Russia and invited him to visit the Ural Mountains. Humboldt was not encouraging about a platinum-based currency, when [silver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silver "Silver") was the standard as a world currency. But the invitation to visit the Urals was intriguing, especially since Humboldt had long dreamed of going to Asia. He had wanted to travel to [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India "India") and made considerable efforts to persuade the [British East India Company](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_East_India_Company "British East India Company") to authorize a trip, but those efforts were fruitless.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015171%E2%80%93174,_199%E2%80%93200-147)

When Russia renewed its earlier invitation to Humboldt, he accepted.[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015199%E2%80%93200-148) The Russians sought to entice Humboldt by engaging his enduring interest in mining sites, for comparative scientific purposes for Humboldt, but for the Russians to gain expert knowledge about their resources. For Humboldt, the Russian monarch's promise to fund the trip was extremely important, since Humboldt's inherited 100,000 thaler fortune was gone and he lived on the Prussian government pension of 2,500–3,000 thalers as the monarch's chamberlain. The Russian government gave an advance of 1200 _chervontsev_ in Berlin and another 20,000 when he arrived in [Saint Petersburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saint_Petersburg "Saint Petersburg").[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Engelhardt60-149)

Humboldt was eager to travel not just to the Urals, but also across the steppes of Siberia to Russia's border with China. Humboldt wrote Cancrin saying that he intended to learn Russian to read mining journals in the language.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955283%E2%80%93285-150) As the details of the expedition were worked out, Humboldt said that he would travel to Russia in his own French coach, with a German servant, as well as [Gustav Rose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gustav_Rose "Gustav Rose"), a professor of chemistry and mineralogy. He also invited [Christian Gottfried Ehrenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Gottfried_Ehrenberg "Christian Gottfried Ehrenberg") to join the expedition, to study water micro-organisms in [Lake Baikal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Baikal "Lake Baikal") and the [Caspian Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caspian_Sea "Caspian Sea"). Humboldt himself was keen to continue his studies of magnetism of mountains and mineral deposits. As was usual for his research, he brought scientific instruments to take the most accurate measurements.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955287-151) The Russians organized the local arrangements, including lodging, horses, accompanying crew. Humboldt's title for the expedition was as an official of the Department of Mines. As the expedition neared dangerous areas, he had to travel in a convoy with an escort.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Engelhardt60-149)

Physically Humboldt was in good condition, despite his advancing years, writing to Cancrin "I still walk very lightly on foot, nine to ten hours without resting, despite my age and my white hair".[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-152)

[![Image 28](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Img079.jpg/250px-Img079.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Img079.jpg)

1959 postage stamp from the Soviet Union

Between May and November 1829 he and the growing expedition traversed the wide expanse of the Russian empire from the [Neva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neva "Neva") to the [Yenisei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yenisei "Yenisei"), accomplishing in twenty-five weeks a distance of 9,614 miles (15,472 km). Humboldt and the expedition party travelled by coach on well maintained roads, with rapid progress being made because of changes of horses at way stations. The party had grown, with Johann Seifert, who was a huntsman and collector of animal specimens; a Russian mining official; Count Adolphe Polier, one of Humboldt's friends from Paris; a cook; plus a contingent of [Cossacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cossacks "Cossacks") for security. Three carriages were filled with people, supplies, and scientific instruments. For Humboldt's magnetic readings to be accurate, they carried an iron-free tent.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015201%E2%80%93202-153) This expedition was unlike his Spanish American travels with Bonpland, with the two alone and sometimes accompanied by local guides. The Russian government was interested in Humboldt's finding prospects for mining and commercial advancement of the realm and made it clear that Humboldt was not to investigate social issues, nor criticize social conditions of Russian [serfs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serfs "Serfs"). In his publications on Spanish America, he did comment on the conditions of the indigenous populations, and deplored black slavery, but well after he had left those territories.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015205-154) As Humboldt discovered, the government kept tight control of the expedition, even when it was 1,000 miles (1,600 km) from Moscow, with local government officials greeting the expedition at every stop. The itinerary was planned with [Tobolsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tobolsk "Tobolsk") the farthest destination, then a return to Saint Petersburg.

Humboldt wrote to the Russian Minister Cancrin that he was extending his travel, knowing that the missive would not reach him in time to scuttle the plan. The further east he journeyed into wilder territory, the more Humboldt enjoyed it. They still followed the Siberian Highway and made excellent progress, sometimes a hundred miles (160 km) in a day.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015206%E2%80%93207-155) Although they were halted at the end of July and warned of an [anthrax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthrax "Anthrax") outbreak, Humboldt decided to continue despite the danger. "At my age, nothing should be postponed".[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-156)

The journey though carried out with all the advantages afforded by the immediate patronage of the Russian government, was too rapid to be profitable scientifically. The correction of the prevalent exaggerated estimate of the height of the Central Asian plateau, and the prediction of the discovery of diamonds in the gold-washings of the Urals, were important aspects of these travels. In the end, the expedition took 8 months, travelled 15,500 km, stopped at 658 post stations, and used 12,244 horses.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEngelhardt190062-157)

One writer claims that "Nothing was quite as Humboldt wanted it. The entire expedition was a compromise."[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015203-158) The Russian emperor offered Humboldt an invitation to return to Russia, but Humboldt declined, due to his disapproval of Nicholas's restrictions on his freedom of movement during the expedition and his ability to freely report on it.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955306-159) Humboldt published two works on the Russian expedition, first _Fragments de géologie et de climatologie asiatiques_ in 1831, based on lectures he gave on the topic. In 1843, he completed the three-volume _Asie Centrale_,[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-160) which he dedicated to Tsar Nicholas, which he called "an unavoidable step, as the expedition was accomplished at his expense".[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-161) As of 2016, these works have not been translated to English.[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015433-162) His 1829 expedition to Russia when he was an old man is much less known than his five-year travels in Spanish America, which had resulted in many published volumes over the decades since his 1804 return. Nevertheless, it gave Humboldt comparative data for his various later scientific publications.

[![Image 29](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg/250px-Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Alexander_von_Humboldt_photo_1857.jpg)

Photograph of Humboldt in his later years

_[Kosmos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmos\_(Humboldt\_book) "Cosmos (Humboldt book)")_ was Humboldt's multi-volume effort in his later years to write a work bringing together all the research from his long career. The writing took shape in [lectures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_lecture "Public lecture") he delivered before the [University of Berlin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humboldt_University_of_Berlin "Humboldt University of Berlin") in the winter of 1827–28. These lectures would form "the [cartoon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cartoon "Cartoon") for the great fresco of the _[K]osmos_".[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-163) His 1829 expedition to Russia supplied him with data comparative to his Latin American expedition.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015235-164)

The first two volumes of the _Kosmos_ were published between the years 1845 and 1847 and were intended to comprise the entire work, but Humboldt published three more volumes, one of which was posthumous. Humboldt had long aimed to write a comprehensive work about geography and the natural sciences. The work attempted to unify the sciences then known in a Kantian framework. With inspiration from [German Romanticism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_Romanticism "German Romanticism"), Humboldt sought to create a compendium of the world's environment.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-Walls_2009_3%E2%80%9315-14) He spent the last decade of his long life—as he called them, his "improbable" years—continuing this work. The third and fourth volumes were published in 1850–58; a fragment of a fifth appeared posthumously in 1862.

His reputation had long since been made with his publications on the Spanish American expedition. There is not a consensus on the importance of _Kosmos_. One scholar, who stresses the importance of Humboldt's _Political Essay on the Kingdom of New Spain_ as essential reading, dismisses _Kosmos_ as "little more than an academic curiosity".[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrading1991534-165) A different opinion is that _Kosmos_ was his "most influential book".[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015235-164)

[![Image 30: First page of the table of contents to volume 1 of "Cosmos," translated by Elise Otté (1849)](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Von_Humboldt-4.jpg/250px-Von_Humboldt-4.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Von_Humboldt-4.jpg)

First page of the table of contents to volume 1 of "Cosmos," translated by [Elise Otté](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elise_Ott%C3%A9 "Elise Otté") (1849)

As with most of Humboldt's works, _Kosmos_ was also translated into multiple languages in editions of uneven quality. It was very popular in Britain and America. In 1849 a German newspaper commented that in England two of the three different translations were made by women, "while in Germany most of the men do not understand it".[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-166) The first translation by Augustin Pritchard—published anonymously by Mr. Baillière (volume I in 1845 and volume II in 1848)—suffered from being hurriedly made. In a letter Humboldt said of it: "It will damage my reputation. All the charm of my description is destroyed by an English sounding like Sanskrit."[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

The other two translations were made by [Elizabeth Juliana Leeves Sabine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elizabeth_Juliana_Leeves_Sabine "Elizabeth Juliana Leeves Sabine") under the superintendence of her husband Col. [Edward Sabine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Sabine "Edward Sabine") (4 volumes 1846–1858), and by [Elise Otté](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elise_Ott%C3%A9 "Elise Otté") (5 volumes 1849–1858, the only complete translation of the 4 German volumes). These three translations were also published in the United States. The numbering of the volumes differs between the German and the English editions. Volume 3 of the German edition corresponds to the volumes 3 and 4 of the English translation, as the German volume appeared in 2 parts in 1850 and 1851. Volume 5 of the German edition was not translated until 1981, again by a woman.[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-167) Otté's translation benefited from a detailed table of contents, and an index for every volume; of the German edition only volumes 4 and 5 had (extremely short) tables of contents, and the index to the whole work only appeared with volume 5 in 1862. Less well known in Germany is the atlas belonging to the German edition of the Cosmos _"Berghaus' Physikalischer Atlas"_, better known as the pirated version by Traugott Bromme under the title _"Atlas zu Alexander von Humboldt's Kosmos"_ (Stuttgart 1861).[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

In Britain, [Heinrich Berghaus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Berghaus "Heinrich Berghaus") planned to publish together with [Alexander Keith Johnston](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Keith_Johnston_(1804%E2%80%931871) "Alexander Keith Johnston (1804–1871)") a _"Physical Atlas"_. But later Johnston published it alone under the title _"The Physical Atlas of Natural Phenomena"_. In Britain its connection to the _Cosmos_ seems not have been recognized.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-168)

[![Image 31](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Muisca_cyphers_acc_acosta_humboldt_zerda.svg/500px-Muisca_cyphers_acc_acosta_humboldt_zerda.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Muisca_cyphers_acc_acosta_humboldt_zerda.svg)

[Muisca numerals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muisca_numerals "Muisca numerals") as noted by Humboldt

Alexander von Humboldt published prolifically throughout his life. Many works were published originally in French or German, then translated to other languages, sometimes with competing translation editions. Humboldt himself did not keep track of all the various editions.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015413-169) He wrote specialized works on particular topics of botany, zoology, astronomy, mineralogy, among others, but he also wrote general works that attracted a wide readership, especially his _Personal Narrative of Travels to the Equinoctial Regions of the New Continent during the years 1799–1804_.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-170) His _Political Essay on the Kingdom of New Spain_ was widely read in Mexico itself, the United States, as well as in Europe.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-171)

Many of the original works have been digitally scanned by the Biodiversity Heritage Library.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-172) There have been new editions of print works, including his _Views of the Cordilleras and Monuments of the Indigenous Peoples of the Americas_ (2014), which includes reproductions of all the color and black and white plates. In the original edition, the publication was in a large format and quite expensive.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKutzinskiEtte2012-173) There is a 2009 translation of his _Geography of Plants_[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-174) and a 2014 English edition of _Views of Nature_.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-175)

Influence on scientists and artists
-----------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alexander_von_Humboldt&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Influence on scientists and artists")]

[![Image 32](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg/250px-Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pickersgill_humboldt.jpg)

Humboldt, portrait by [Henry William Pickersgill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_William_Pickersgill "Henry William Pickersgill") (1831)

Humboldt was generous toward his friends and mentored young scientists. He and Bonpland parted ways after their return to Europe, and Humboldt largely took on the task of publishing the results of their Latin American expedition at Humboldt's expense, but he included Bonpland as co-author on the nearly 30 published volumes. Bonpland returned to Latin America, settling in Buenos Aires, Argentina, then moved to the countryside near the border with [Paraguay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paraguay "Paraguay"). The forces of Dr. [José Gaspar Rodríguez de Francia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Gaspar_Rodr%C3%ADguez_de_Francia "José Gaspar Rodríguez de Francia"), the strong man of Paraguay, abducted Bonpland after killing Bonpland's estate workers. Bonpland was accused of "agricultural espionage" and of threatening Paraguay's virtual monopoly on the cultivation of [yerba mate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yerba_mate "Yerba mate").

Despite international pressure, including the British government and Simón Bolívar's, along with European scientists including Humboldt, Francia kept Bonpland prisoner until 1831. He was released after nearly 10 years in Paraguay. Humboldt and Bonpland maintained a warm correspondence about science and politics until Bonpland's death in 1858.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015272-176)

During Humboldt's time in Paris, he met in 1818 the young and brilliant Peruvian student of the Royal Mining School of Paris, [Mariano Eduardo de Rivero y Ustariz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mariano_Eduardo_de_Rivero_y_Ustariz "Mariano Eduardo de Rivero y Ustariz"). Subsequently, Humboldt acted as a mentor of the career of this promising Peruvian scientist. Another recipient of Humboldt's aid was [Louis Agassiz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Agassiz "Louis Agassiz") (1807–1873), who was directly aided with needed cash from Humboldt, assistance in securing an academic position, and help with getting his research on zoology published. Agassiz sent him copies of his publications and went on to gain considerable scientific recognition as a professor at Harvard.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955326%E2%80%93327-177) Agassiz delivered an address to the Boston Society of Natural History in 1869, on the centenary of his patron's birth.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-178) When Humboldt was an elderly man, he aided another young scholar, [Gotthold Eisenstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gotthold_Eisenstein "Gotthold Eisenstein"), a brilliant, young, Jewish mathematician in Berlin, for whom he obtained a small crown pension and whom he nominated for the Academy of Science.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Terra1955334%E2%80%93336-179)

Humboldt's popular writings inspired many scientists and naturalists, including [Charles Darwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Darwin "Charles Darwin"), [Henry David Thoreau](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau "Henry David Thoreau"), [John Muir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Muir "John Muir"), [George Perkins Marsh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Perkins_Marsh "George Perkins Marsh"), [Ernst Haeckel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel "Ernst Haeckel"),[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-180)[Ida Laura Pfeiffer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ida_Laura_Pfeiffer "Ida Laura Pfeiffer")[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-181) as well as brothers [Richard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Schomburgk "Richard Schomburgk") and [Robert Schomburgk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Schomburgk "Robert Schomburgk")[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKutzinskiEtte2012xxiv-182) and [Robert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_von_Schlagintweit "Robert von Schlagintweit"), [Adolf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adolf_Schlagintweit "Adolf Schlagintweit"), and [Hermann Schlagintweit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermann_von_Schlagintweit "Hermann von Schlagintweit").[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-183)

Humboldt carried on correspondence with many contemporaries and two volumes of letters to [Karl August Varnhagen von Ense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_August_Varnhagen_von_Ense "Karl August Varnhagen von Ense") have been published.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-184)[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-185)

Charles Darwin made frequent reference to Humboldt's work in his _[Voyage of the Beagle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Voyage\_of\_the\_Beagle "The Voyage of the Beagle")_, where Darwin described his own scientific exploration of the Americas. In one note, he placed Humboldt first on the "list of American travellers".[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-186) Darwin's work was influenced by Humboldt's writing style as well. Darwin's sister remarked to him "you had, probably from reading so much of Humboldt, got his phraseology and the kind of flowery French expressions he uses".[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf2015226-187)

When Darwin's _Journal_ was published, he sent a copy to Humboldt, who responded, "You told me in your kind letter that, when you were young, the manner in which I studied and depicted nature in the torrid zones contributed toward exciting in you the ardour and desire to travel in distant lands. Considering the importance of your work, Sir, this may be the greatest success that my humble work could bring."[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-188) In his autobiography, Darwin recalled, reading "with care and profound interest Humboldt's _Personal Narrative_" and finding it one of the two most influential books on his work, which stirred in him "a burning zeal to add even the most humble contribution to the noble structure of Natural Science".[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-189)

Humboldt would later reveal to Darwin in the 1840s that he had been deeply interested in Darwin's grandfather's poetry. [Erasmus Darwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erasmus_Darwin "Erasmus Darwin") had published the poem [_The Loves of the Plants_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Botanic_Garden "The Botanic Garden") in the early 1800s. Humboldt praised the poem for combining nature and imagination, a theme that permeated Humboldt's own work.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201537-190)

[![Image 33](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg/250px-Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Church_Heart_of_the_Andes.jpg)

[Frederic Edwin Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederic_Edwin_Church "Frederic Edwin Church"), _[The Heart of the Andes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Heart\_of\_the\_Andes "The Heart of the Andes")_ (1859)

A number of nineteenth-century artists travelled to Latin America, following in the footsteps of Humboldt, painting landscapes and scenes of everyday life. [Johann Moritz Rugendas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Moritz_Rugendas "Johann Moritz Rugendas"), [Ferdinand Bellermann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferdinand_Bellermann "Ferdinand Bellermann"), and [Eduard Hildebrandt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eduard_Hildebrandt "Eduard Hildebrandt") were three important European painters.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-191)[Frederic Edwin Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederic_Edwin_Church "Frederic Edwin Church") was the most famous landscape painter in the U.S. in the nineteenth century. His paintings of Andean volcanoes that Humboldt climbed helped make Church's reputation. His 5 foot by 10 foot painting entitled _[The Heart of the Andes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Heart\_of\_the\_Andes "The Heart of the Andes")_ "caused a sensation" when it was completed. Church had hoped to ship the painting to Berlin to show the painting to Humboldt, but Humboldt died a few days after Church's letter was written.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-192)[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-193)[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-194)[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-195) Church painted [Cotopaxi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cotopaxi "Cotopaxi") three times, twice in 1855 and then in 1859 in eruption.

[George Catlin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Catlin "George Catlin"), most famous for his portraits of North American Indians and paintings of life among various North American tribes, also travelled to South America, producing a number of paintings. He wrote to Humboldt in 1855, sending him his proposal for South American travels. Humboldt replied, thanking him and sending a memorandum helping guide his travels.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-196)[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-197)

[Ida Laura Pfeiffer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ida_Laura_Pfeiffer "Ida Laura Pfeiffer"), one of the first female travelers who completed two trips around the world from 1846 to 1855, followed in Humboldt's footsteps. The two explorers met in Berlin in 1851 before Pfeiffer's second tour and again in 1855 when she returned to Europe. Humboldt provided Pfeiffer with an open letter of introduction in which he bade anyone who knew of his name to assist Madame Pfeiffer for her "inextinguishable energy of character which she has everywhere shown, to wheresoever's she has been called or better put, driven by her unconquerable passion to study nature and man."[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt#cite_note-198)
